# DA --- Energy --- CMSW

### File Notes

Thanks to Carlota, Ilana, Isha, Jason, Maeve, Nishant, Sam, Savanna,
Wenxi, & Xavier for their hard work on this file!

[The story of the Energy DA:]{.underline}

Uniqueness -- the fossil fuel industry is strong, strong investments in
it now

Link -- the plan creates a seismic departure from the status quo that
reduces fossil fuel demand (either by increasing renewables,
geoengineering, nuclear power via FNPPs, etc.)

Internal Link + Impact -- Reducing fossil fuel demand destroys the
fossil fuel industry (profits, investments) which is bad for things like
energy security, the economy, or the agriculture industry.

It is meant to be read against these affirmatives at camp so far: FNPPs,
Geoengineering (multiple versions)

### 

### DA --- 1NC

**The fossil fuel industry is strong now --- investment proves, LNG
projects increasing. Only policy pressure like the plan can change
this.**

**Gustin 6-17 ---** Georgina Gustin, Reporter, has covered intersections
of farming, food systems and the environment for much of her journalism
career. Awards: John B. Oakes Award for Distinguished Environmental
Journalism, and she was twice named the Glenn Cunningham Agricultural
Journalist of the Year, once with ICN. She is a graduate of the Columbia
University Graduate School of Journalism and the University of Colorado
at Boulder, 2025 ("The World's Banks are Still Betting on Fossil Fuel
Industry Growth." June 17,
<https://insideclimatenews.org/news/17062025/banks-continue-to-back-fossil-fuel-industry/>
Accessed: 7/14/25)

**[The [world's biggest banks]{.mark} continue to [bankroll]{.mark} the
[expansion of]{.mark} the [fossil fuel industry]{.mark} and have largely
retreated from their climate commitments]{.underline}**, even as the
world heads toward breaching thresholds for a livable planet.

**[A new report from a coalition of environmental and banking advocacy
groups, published Tuesday, finds that the [world's]{.mark} 65 [biggest
banks committed \$869 billion to fossil fuels]{.mark} companies
[in]{.mark} 20[24]{.mark}---a jump of \$162 billion over 2023. A big
chunk of that---[\$429 billion]{.mark}---went [to companies that
have]{.mark} specific [plans to expand their development]{.mark} of
fossil fuels.]{.underline}**

The report looks in detail at the years since 2021, when scientists with
the International Energy Agency (IEA) said fossil fuel expansion
effectively had to stop in order to meet global climate targets.

"The \[IEA\] put out a very comprehensive study that looked at the
energy roadmap for getting to net-zero emissions by 2050 globally," said
Allison Fajans-Turner, a researcher with the Rainforest Action Network
and a co-author of the report. "One of the major findings was there was
no room for any upstream, midstream or downstream fossil fuel
infrastructure to be built, burned or combusted if we are serious about
limiting temperature rise to 1.5 degrees. **[[Since then]{.mark},
unfortunately, we've seen [banks put \$1.6 trillion into fossil fuel
expansion]{.mark}."]{.underline}**

**[American banks are leading the pack, the new report finds. JPMorgan
Chase, Bank of America and Citigroup are the top three financial
supporters of fossil fuel companies globally. Along with Wells Fargo,
which ranks fifth after the Japanese bank Mizuho, these [four banks
represent more than 20 percent of]{.mark} total [global fossil fuel
financing]{.mark}, the report found.]{.underline}**

The analysis, by the Banking on Climate Chaos coalition, draws on
financial databases used by the banking industry and from annual company
reports, stock filings and presentations. It looks at roughly 2,700
companies engaged in fossil fuel development or distribution.

[**The [\$162 billion jump]{.mark} from 2023 to 2024 [reverses]{.mark} a
[downward trend]{.mark} that was caused by a combination of factors, the
authors say. Energy prices increased following [Russia's
invasion]{.mark} of Ukraine, [boost]{.mark}ing the [fossil fuel
industry's profits]{.mark}** and decreasing its need for financing and
loans]{.underline}. Since then, central banks' interest rate cuts have
made [loans cheaper and more attractive]{.underline}, potentially
prompting banks to borrow more. 

The broader political climate could also be a factor, as regulators,
including those in the **[[U.S., retreat on climate
rules]{.underline}]{.mark}**. The Securities and Exchange Commission
decided to drop its pursuit of climate disclosure rules in March.

"It's a [combination of factors---some political, some market-based and
some really successful campaigning by the fossil fuel
industry]{.underline} on anti-ESG," said Jessye Waxman, a researcher
with the Sierra Club, who was one of the new report's authors. 

**[The [global expansion of]{.mark} liquified natural gas [(LNG)]{.mark}
is [also a factor]{.mark}. "We know that we're seeing one of [the
biggest fossil fuel build-out in the LNG sector]{.mark}]{.underline}**,"
said Caleb Schwartz, also with the Rainforest Action Network and an
author of the report. "We can point to the gulf in the U.S. south with
all these huge, capital-hungry profits. The banks are set up to profit
from it."

Regardless of the drivers, the report says the increase represents a
worrying trend, especially after banks had started making stronger
commitments to slow financing for fossil fuels. In 2021, with backing
from the United Nations, the **[[Net Zero Bank Alliance]{.mark} launched
with the [aim of aligning banks']{.mark} lending and underwriting to the
goal enshrined [in]{.mark} the [Paris Agreement]{.mark}]{.underline}**
of keeping global temperature rise to 1.5 degrees compared to
pre-industrial levels. 

At the annual United Nations climate conference held in Glasgow that
year, many of the world's major banks joined the alliance, suggesting
banks were serious about their climate goals by adhering to an ambitious
global commitment.

**[[But]{.mark} earlier this year [all major]{.mark} American, Canadian
and Japanese [members]{.mark} of the alliance [pulled out]{.mark},
according to the new report. [Less than half of the]{.mark} 65
[banks]{.mark} analyzed in the report [were still
members]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

**[Even before the banks left the alliance, it had already [watered down
its goals]{.mark} from urging banks to align their financing with a 1.5
degree target [to]{.mark} a [less ambitious 2 degree]{.mark} target. The
reports' authors stress that voluntary [commitments are failing]{.mark},
and say the only viable way to slow fossil fuel financing is through
central bank and regulatory policies.]{.underline}**

**["[As long as there's no policy pressure]{.mark} to derail them from
their models, they won't do it," said Katrin Ganswindt, a researcher
with the German research firm Urgewald, a member of the coalition.
"[They won't change course]{.mark}."]{.underline}**

**Strong fossil fuel industry is key to energy security** (& status quo
solves sustainability)

**Kraidman 24** --- Gary Kraidman has a Master of Science degree in
microbiology and Bachelor of Science degree from Brooklyn College, 2024
(\"The Role of Oil and Gas in Global Energy Security,\" February 4,
https://energiesmedia.com/the-role-of-oil-and-gas-in-global-energy-security/
//wenxi)

The **[industrial revolution, which propelled societies into modernity,
was fueled by the abundant and versatile energy derived from [oil and
gas]{.mark}. From powering factories to facilitating transportation,
these [resources]{.mark} became [the backbone of economic
progress]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Nations with substantial oil and gas
reserves gained strategic advantages, establishing themselves as key
players on the global stage.

**[Ensuring Energy Stability]{.underline}**

**[[One of the most significant contributions]{.mark} of the oil and gas
industry [to global energy security is]{.mark} the provision of [stable
and consistent energy sources]{.mark}. [Unlike renewable energy]{.mark},
which is often subject to weather conditions and geographical
limitations, oil and gas can be extracted and utilized consistently,
providing a reliable energy foundation. This [stability is crucial
for]{.mark} meeting [the ever-growing energy demands]{.mark} of a
burgeoning global population.]{.underline}**

**[Geopolitical Dynamics And Energy Security]{.underline}**

**[The distribution of [oil and gas]{.mark} reserves across the globe
[has profound implications for geopolitical dynamics]{.mark}. Nations
endowed with abundant resources often become key players in
international relations, shaping alliances and influencing global
policies.]{.underline}** At the same time, dependency on oil and gas
imports can create vulnerabilities, highlighting the delicate balance
between energy security and geopolitical stability.

Technological Advancements

**[[The]{.mark} oil and gas [industry]{.mark} has continually evolved,
[embracing tech]{.mark}nological advancements [that]{.mark} enhance
efficiency, [reduce environmental impact]{.mark}, and unlock new
reserves. [Innovations]{.mark} in exploration, extraction, and refining
processes have not only increased the availability of these resources
but have also [made them more sustainable. This]{.mark} commitment to
technological progress [aligns with the broader goal of ensuring
long-term global energy security.]{.mark}]{.underline}**

**Energy security is key to stability, otherwise great power war.**

**Wang et al 24 ---** Dr. Qiang Wang has a Ph.D in Environmental
Science. Fen Ren is a graduate student at China University of Petroleum,
East China, Qingdao. Dr. Rongrong Li is a professor at China University
of Petroleum (East China), 2024 (\"Geopolitics and energy security: a
comprehensive exploration of evolution, collaborations, and future
directions,\" Humanit Soc Sci Commun 11, 1071 (2024).
https://doi.org/10.1057/s41599-024-03507-2 Accessed: 7/21/25)

**[[Energy,]{.mark} as a productive resource, is essential to ensuring
the productive lives of the country's citizens, it is also a strategic
and politically attributed resource and [plays an important role
in]{.mark} ensuring national [security and]{.mark} socio-economic
[stability]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Yang et al., 2022). As the world's
industrialization process accelerates, technological advances and
industrial expansion continue to drive social development, the extensive
demand for energy resources has triggered global concerns about energy
security. The concept of energy security is initially concerned with
ensuring an uninterrupted and reliable supply of energy to meet a
country's or region's production needs. However, despite the importance
of this issue, there is still no consensus among academics on a
definition of energy security. This is because the concept depends on
the contextual background and the different national settings (Kruyt et
al., 2009). The scope of energy security is not limited to energy supply
but also encompasses the stability of energy markets, the connectivity
of global energy supply chains, and the sustainability of energy
resources. **[Energy security is of paramount importance to the economic
stability and growth of countries and regions. [A stable energy
supply]{.mark} is the foundation for [sustain]{.mark}ing industrial
[production, transportation, and]{.mark} daily [life. Any
disruption]{.mark} in energy supply or sharp price fluctuations [will
result in higher]{.mark} production [costs and]{.mark} increased
[inflation]{.mark}, thereby [affecting economic growth and social
stability]{.mark}. In addition, energy security is an important
component of national security. Disruptions or [shortfalls in energy
supplies]{.mark} can lead to social unrest and [affect national
security]{.mark} (Sivaram and Saha, 2018). Therefore, [energy]{.mark}
[security is the key]{.mark} [to]{.mark} sustaining economic growth[,
ensuring political stability]{.mark}, and promoting social
well-being]{.underline}** (Lee et al., 2022). The factors affecting
energy security are multifaceted, among which the impact of geopolitical
risks on energy security cannot be ignored. Geopolitics is defined as
the risks associated with war, terrorism, and inter-State tensions that
affects the normalization of international relations and the peace
process (Lee and Wang, 2021). First of all, the political stability of
energy-supplying countries has a direct impact on the reliability of
their energy exports. Factors such as political instability, civil
unrest, and war can lead to disruptions in energy production and
transportation, thus threatening the stability of the global energy
supply chain. **[For instance, instances of political unrest and
conflict in the [Mid]{.mark}dle [East]{.mark} frequently resulted in
[disruptions to the oil supply]{.mark}, which in turn [gave rise
to]{.mark} pronounced [fluctuations in the price of oil on the
international market]{.mark} (Ben Cheikh and Ben Zaied, 2023). The
ongoing conflict between Russia and Ukraine has also resulted in
significant fluctuations in the prices of oil and gas (Zhao et al.,
2023]{.underline}**). Secondly, the establishment and maintenance of
diplomatic relations between countries also have a significant impact on
energy security. International sanctions, trade disputes, and diplomatic
conflicts may restrict energy imports or exports, thereby exposing
countries that are dependent on imported energy to the risk of supply
shortages and price increases (Zhang et al., **[2024). [The relationship
between]{.mark} [Russia and the West served as an]{.mark} illustrative
[example]{.mark} of the manner in which geopolitical tensions can give
rise to [increased uncertainty]{.mark} regarding the supply of gas,
which in turn [affected Europe's energy security]{.mark}]{.underline}**
(Slakaityte et al., 2023). In addition, geopolitical risks include the
security of energy transportation corridors, such as security threats to
maritime transportation routes (Desogus et al., 2023). A significant
disruption to the global energy market would result from the threat or
actual blocking of important transportation corridors, such as the
Strait of Hormuz or the Strait of Malacca (Meza et al.,
2022[).]{.underline}

### UQ --- Fossil Fuel Industry Strong --- 2NC/1NR

#### Oil demand and output are high

**Astakhova & Soldatkin 7/14** (Olesya Astakhova graduated at Plekhanov
Academy journalist for Reuters and Vladimir Soldatkin reporter at
Thomson Reuters) 7/14/2025 OPEC projects \'very strong\' third-quarter
oil demand, followed by tight balance, RIA reports.
https://www.reuters.com/business/energy/opec-projects-very-strong-third-quarter-oil-demand-followed-by-tight-balance-ria-2025-07-14//Bend

**[[OPEC]{.underline}]{.mark}**, which along with its allies is
**[[ramping up oil output, expects \"very strong\" oil
demand]{.underline}]{.mark}** in the third quarter and a
**[[tight]{.mark} supply-demand [balance in]{.mark} the [following
months]{.mark}]{.underline}**, its secretary general said, according to
a Russian media report.

The eight members of **[the [OPEC+ producer
group]{.mark}]{.underline}**, comprising the Organization of the
Petroleum Exporting Countries and allies including Russia,
are **[[unwinding years of cuts]{.mark} that had been [aimed at
supporting the market]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

Five sources told Reuters that **[[OPEC]{.mark}+ oil
producers]{.underline}** are set to **[[approve another big output
boost]{.underline}]{.mark}** for September.

Russia\'s RIA news agency quoted Haitham Al Ghais on Monday as telling
journalists on the sidelines of last week\'s **[OPEC]{.underline}**
seminar in Vienna that the organisation **[[expected demand growth of
1.3 million barrels per day]{.mark} year on year in 2025 [due to]{.mark}
a [strong global economy]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

\"And that means **[we are seeing]{.underline}**, especially in the
third quarter, **[very strong demand growth]{.underline}**,\" he said,
according to the report.

\"In the fourth quarter also we\'re seeing good demand growth, and the
**[balances will be tight.]{.underline}** And this is one of the main
fundamental factors that is leading for the group of **[[eight
countries]{.mark} to [bring barrels back to]{.mark} the
[market]{.mark}]{.underline}**,\" he added.

Al Ghais\' comments come as OPEC trimmed its global oil demand forecasts
for the next four years last week on slowing Chinese growth, even as it
lifted its longer-term view based on rising consumption in the
developing world.

Global demand will average **[[105 million barrels per day this
year]{.underline}]{.mark}**, OPEC said in its 2025 World Oil
Outlook published on Thursday. It **[[expects]{.mark} demand [to
grow]{.mark}]{.underline}** to average 106.3 million bpd in 2026 and
then climb **[[to 111.6 million bpd]{.underline}]{.mark}** in 2029.

#### Increased use of fossil fuels and sharp decreases in renewables lock in fossil fuel heg. 

**Maguire 7/10** (Gavin Maguire is the Global Energy Transition
Columnist. He was previously Asia Commodities and Energy editor.
Graduated from mendoza college of buiness) 7/10/25 Fossil fuels show
staying power as EU clean energy output dips: Maguire,
https://www.tradingview.com/news/reuters.com%2C2025%3Anewsml_L1N3T703S%3A0-fossil-fuels-show-staying-power-as-eu-clean-energy-output-dips-maguire//Bend

Utilities across the **[[E]{.mark}uropean [U]{.mark}nion [cranked output
from n]{.mark}atural [g]{.mark}as [and coal-fired power
plants]{.mark}]{.underline}** during the opening half of 2025,
**[boosting power sector emissions and [reversing]{.mark} recent [energy
transition momentum]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

The upswing in fossil production follows two years of steep declines in
fossil use within the EU, which established Europe as a global leader in
efforts to curb reliance on polluting fuels in power production.

However, **[[year-on-year drops in]{.mark} output from [wind farms and
hydro dams deprived EU]{.mark} utilities]{.underline}** of key sources
of clean power during January to June, **[[and forced them to compensate
with]{.mark} sharply [higher]{.mark} generation from [fossil fuel
plants]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

\<Graph omitted\>

This **[abrupt [reversal in EU fossil fuel use highlights the
challenge]{.mark}]{.underline}** facing even modern energy systems when
weather patterns impede clean power supplies, and suggests that
**[[fossil fuels]{.mark} may [stay rooted in global power systems for
years more]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

CLIMATE CHANGED

**[[EU utilities generated 13% more]{.mark} electricity [from fossil
fuels]{.mark}]{.underline}** during January to June compared to the same
period in 2024, **[[which was the largest annual increase]{.mark} in
fossil output]{.underline}** for that period since 2017, data from think
tank Ember showed.

**[[Gas]{.mark}-fired generation [climbed by 19%]{.mark} to the [highest
in three years]{.mark}, while [coal]{.mark}-fired output [rose by 2% to
two-year highs]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

The higher gas and coal combustion resulted in **[[power emissions
rising by 9%]{.underline}]{.mark}** from the year before to 297 million
metric tons of carbon dioxide, and means that the **[EU could discharge
nearly 600 million tons of CO2 this year]{.underline}** if the current
burn pace is sustained.

The main triggers of the higher fossil fuel use were steep declines in
clean power supplies tied to weather conditions across Europe since late
2024.

\<Graph emitted\>

**[[Output from wind farms]{.mark},]{.underline}** which accounted for
close to 20% of EU electricity supplies during the first half of 2024,
**[[registered]{.mark} the [largest year-over-year
fall]{.mark}]{.underline}** on record during January to June,
**[[dropping by 9%]{.underline}]{.mark}** to 225 terawatt hours (TWh).

Sustained low wind speeds at turbine level - especially in Germany which
is home to over 30% of EU wind farm capacity - were the main drag on
wind power production.

At the same time, below-expected snow and rainfall over the winter in
Europe resulted in a 15% year-on-year drop in electricity output from
hydro dams, which accounted for around 15% of EU electricity supplies
last year.

The roughly 164 TWh of hydro electricity output during January to June
was 28 TWh less than during the same months in 2024, and was the lowest
in two years for that period.

**[[SOLAR SHINES - BUT NOT ENOUGH]{.underline}]{.mark}**

A 21% or 32 TWh increase in EU solar power electricity supplies helped
provide some cushioning for utilities against the drops in wind and
hydro generation.

The 179 TWh of electricity output from EU solar farms during the first
half of 2025 was a record, and marked the first time that the EU\'s
solar farms produced more electricity than the region\'s hydro dams
during the January to June window.

\<Graph omitted\>

And steady increases in installed solar capacity look set to lift
solar-powered electricity supplies to new heights over the coming
months, ensuring that clean energy advocates in the EU will have
something to celebrate.

However, the deep and enduring drops to wind and hydro output are also a
major cause for concern, especially as climate trends indicate that
further deviations in weather patterns from previous averages can be
expected.

\<Graph omitted\>

Steadily rising average temperatures are resulting in steadily declining
snow coverage at low altitudes across Europe, and are pinching regional
hydro dam output - despite hydro dam capacity in the EU hitting a record
last year.

And while climate change is triggering more intense storms in many
areas, it is also causing more frequent wind droughts as the temperature
differential between the Earth\'s poles and tropical areas narrows.

This phenomenon - known as Global Stilling - is a major worry for energy
planners who have been banking on large scale wind farms playing a
dependable role in clean electricity generation over the coming decades.

If wind speeds regularly slow to a crawl for months on end, and hydro
networks continue to get only meagre snow and rainfall during the
winters**[, [EU energy suppliers]{.mark} will [struggle]{.mark} to keep
up [with demand]{.mark} - [even if solar output]{.mark} keeps
[climb]{.mark}ing.]{.underline}**

**[That in turn [means]{.mark} that [regardless of long-term ambitions
to wean power systems off fossil fuels, natural gas and coal
will]{.mark} likely [remain integral]{.mark} tools [for EU]{.mark}
utilities [for the foreseeable future]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

### UQ --- Arctic LNG high --- 2NC/1NR

**Arctic LNG demand is high now**

Tim **Jay 25**. Reporter at Global Trade Magazine. "U.S. LNG Exports to
Europe Surge Amid High Demand and Cold Weather." 3/5/25.
https://www.globaltrademag.com/u-s-lng-exports-to-europe-surge-amid-high-demand-and-cold-weather/

**[Cold weather and attractive pricing have driven Europe's [renewed
appetite for U.S. LNG]{.mark}, [reinforcing]{.mark} the [U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates' [position as]{.mark} the world's [top]{.mark} LNG
[exporter]{.mark} a remarkable ascent just over a decade after entering
the market.]{.underline}**

**[The trend may [signal]{.mark} a [shift back toward U.S.
supply]{.mark} after a challenging 2024, when European imports of
American LNG dropped 18%, while Russian shipments mainly from the Yamal
LNG plant grew by the same percentage. [Russia also set a new record for
Arctic LNG exports, moving nearly 22 million tonnes via the Northern Sea
Route.]{.mark}]{.underline}**

**Arctic LNG demand high now --- investment**

**IEA 22** (IEA, The International Energy Agency works with governments
and industry to shape a secure and sustainable energy future for all) 21
March 2022, Energy Fact Sheet: Why does Russian oil and gas matter?
https://www.iea.org/articles/energy-fact-sheet-why-does-russian-oil-and-gas-matter

A giant in natural gas markets

**[[Russia]{.mark} is the world's second-largest producer of natural
gas, behind the United States, and [has the world's largest gas
reserves]{.mark}. Russia [is the]{.mark} world's [largest gas
exporter]{.mark}. In 2021 the country produced 762 bcm of natural gas,
and exported approximately 210 bcm via pipeline.]{.underline}**

Gazprom and Novatek are Russia's main gas producers, but many Russian
oil companies, including Rosneft, also operate gas production
facilities. Gazprom, which is state-owned, is the largest gas producer,
but its share of production has declined over the past decade, as
Novatek and Rosneft have expanded their production capacity. However,
[Gazprom still accounted for 68% of Russian gas production in
2021.]{.underline} Historically, production was concentrated in West
Siberia, **[but [investment has shifted in the past decade to]{.mark}
Yamal and Eastern Siberia and the Far East, as well as [the offshore
Arctic]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

Russia also has a wide-reaching gas export pipeline network, both via
transit routes through Belarus and Ukraine, and via pipelines sending
gas directly into Europe (including  Nord Stream,  Blue Stream, and
TurkStream pipelines) all. Russia completed work on the Nord Stream II
pipeline in 2021, but the German government decided not to approve
certification in the wake of the Russian invasion of Ukraine**[. [Russia
natural gas accounted for 45% of imports and almost 40% of European
Union gas demand]{.mark} in 2021. This share has increased in recent
years]{.underline}**, as European domestic natural gas production has
declined. Germany, Turkey and Italy are the largest importers of Russian
natural gas.

In late 2019, Russia launched a major eastward gas export pipeline, the
roughly 3,000 km-long Power of Siberia pipeline, which has a capacity of
38 bcm, in order to be able to send gas from far east fields directly to
China. In 2021, Gazprom exported over 10 bcm of natural gas via the
Power of Siberia pipeline, with exports set to gradually ramp-up to 38
bcm in the coming years. Russia is looking to develop the Power of
Siberia-2 pipeline, with a capacity of 50 bcm/y, which would supply
China from the West Siberian gas fields. No supply agreements and no
final investment decision have yet been reached on the pipeline, which
would further lessen Russia's  reliance on European customers for gas.

Furthermore, **[[Russia has been expanding its]{.mark} liquefied natural
gas [(LNG) capacity]{.mark}, in order to compete with growing LNG
exports from the United States, Australia and Qatar. In 2021, the
government released a long-term LNG development plan, targeting 110-190
bcm/year LNG exports by 2025. In 2021, Russia exported 40 bcm of LNG,
[making it the world's 4th largest LNG exporter]{.mark} and [accounting
for approximately 8% of global LNG]{.mark} supply. ]{.underline}**

**[In recent years, [Russia has increasingly focused on the Arctic as a
way to increase oil and gas production]{.mark} and offset declines at
existing and older production sites. [The Arctic accounts for over 80%
of Russia's n]{.mark}atural [g]{.mark}as [production and]{.mark} an
estimated [20%  of its crude production]{.mark}.]{.underline}** While
climate change threatens future investment in the region, it also
presents Russia with the opportunity of increasing access to Arctic
trade routes, allowing for further flexibility for seaborne deliveries
of fossil fuels, particularly to Asia. 

### AT: Biden Thumper --- 2NC/1NR

**Biden tried to kill the Fossil Fuel industry with the largest clean
energy investment in history, instead it reached a 10 year high**

**Delouya 24**, Samantha Delouya is a writer for CNN Business covering
housing, labor issues, and breaking business and economics news. She has
written about everything from legal matters in the business world to
stories revealing the real-world impact of shifts in the economy.Before
joining CNN in 2023, Delouya was a senior reporter at Business Insider.
She began her journalism career as a television producer at CNBC, where
she worked for six years: first as an assignment desk producer and later
as a markets producer.Delouya was born and raised in New York but is
currently based in Los Angeles. She is a graduate of Cornell University
where she studied industrial and labor relations. "Why oil companies are
raking in record profits under Joe Biden," June 11 2024.
[[https://www.cnn.com/2024/06/11/economy/oil-industry-profits-under-biden]{.underline}](https://www.cnn.com/2024/06/11/economy/oil-industry-profits-under-biden)
istone

Nearly four years ago, while campaigning in the runup to the last
presidential election, Donald Trump warned that President Joe Biden
would "destroy" the oil industry.As Trump and Biden are set once again
to face off in a presidential election, Trump has renewed claims that
Biden's agenda has hurt energy producers, promising to hit rewind on
Biden's environmental policies.But [**[the oil and gas industry in the
U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates []{.mark}has
[thrived]{.mark}**]{.underline} under the current president, **[[even
as]{.mark} [the Biden administration has touted]{.mark} its
[efforts]{.mark} [to transitio]{.mark}n away from fossil fuels and
[towards green energy]{.mark}]{.underline}** sources.In the last three
and a half years[, US oil production --- and **[oil and gas company
profits]{.mark}** --- **[have broken records]{.mark}**.The top five
US-based oil and gas companies by market cap, according to S&P Global
--- ExxonMobil (XOM), Chevron (CVX), ConocoPhillips (CPP), EOG Resources
(EOG) and Schlumberger (SLB) --- have raked in more than \$250 billion
in profits between 2021 and 2023. That's a 160% jump]{.underline}
compared to the first three years of the pro-big-oil Trump

industry's recent profit** windfall **underscores the limited influence
of any US president**]{.underline} --- whether in favor of fossil fuel

for most \[oil\] companies," said Kloza.According to the US Energy

2023]{.underline}, beating the previous record set in 2019 --- and
Investors have been richly rewarded. [The Energy Select Sector SPDR Fund
(XLE), which tracks [**the performance** **of**]{.mark} **the largest
[oil and gas companies, is up more than 100% since Biden's
inauguration]{.mark}**]{.underline}.The cash-rich industry has not shied

form of dividends. In 2023, ExxonMobil was the third-largest dividend
payer in the S&P 500, behind only Microsoft and Apple, according to
ExxonMobil CEO Darren Woods.

[Many oil CEOs have also grown their own fortunes during the Biden
administration. Chevron CEO Mike Wirth saw his compensation grow 17%,
from \$22.6 million in 2021 to \$26.5 million in 2023.]{.underline}
Exxon CEO Woods' compensation rose an eye-popping 56% in that span, from
\$23.6 million in 2021 to \$36.9 million in 2023.In his first term,

**[Uncertainty]{.underline} from Trump's [funding freezes]{.underline}

**Naiden '25** \[Alena; January 24; Alaska desk reporter; Alaska Public

rural Alaska in limbo,"
https://alaskapublic.org/news/alaska-desk/2025-01-24/trump-executive-order-puts-dozens-of-clean-energy-projects-in-rural-alaska-in-limbo\]

**[Over [\$130 million in grants]{.mark} allocated [to clean
energy]{.mark} projects [in]{.mark} rural [Alaska]{.mark} are now
[frozen]{.mark}, [following]{.mark} one of President Donald [Trump's
ex]{.mark}ecutive [o]{.mark}rders. The [projects]{.mark} -- which
[include]{.mark} hydroelectric [dams]{.mark}, wind [turbines and
solar]{.mark} energy systems]{.underline}** -- aim to lower energy costs
in rural communities and bring them additional revenue to support
crucial infrastructure.

Trump signed dozens of executive orders during his first day in office,
including one that freezes funding from the federal Inflation Reduction
Act and Bipartisan Infrastructure Law. A section of that order mandated
that federal agencies pause the disbursement of funds appropriated
through those acts.

[**That's [created uncertainty for]{.mark} many Alaska [projects]{.mark}
--- including two large wind turbines** slated to be built in
Kotzebue]{.underline}. Those turbines, along with the community's
existing renewable energy assets, would allow the community to produce
more than half of their energy from solar and wind, said Chad Nordlum,
the energy project manager for the Native Village of Kotzebue. He said
it's unclear now if the funding freeze will affect the project's
timeline.

**"[The freeze that Trump has put on the Inflation Reduction Act has
[definitely caused a lot of concern]{.mark} for Kotzebue for the wind
project]{.underline}," Nordlum said. "[It puts a [lot of questions out
there]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Right now, there doesn\'t seem to be much
we can do on our end. We\'re just waiting for clarity."

How freeze affects projects in progress

The Alaska Native Tribal Health Consortium has partnered with tribes
across the state, including in Kotzebue, to receive the federal grants.
In total, they've been awarded contracts for about \$132 million, said
Katya Karankevich, a project manager at ANTHC's Department of
Environmental Health and Engineering.

The contracts covered hydroelectric dams in Old Harbor and Chignik Bay;
wind turbines in Kotzebue, Toksook Bay and Chevak; and solar energy
systems in Ouzinkie, Brevig Mission, Elim, Koyuk, Savoonga and Teller.
ANTHC also received about \$32 million for their own grant program for
solar, which would cover between nine and 12 additional communities.

And ANTHC has already started spending or obligating funds with
contractors on 30% of the projects, Karankevich said. **[[With]{.mark}
the federal [funding freeze]{.mark}, the consortium now [cannot request
reimbursement for the work already started]{.mark}]{.underline}**, she
said.

"We will have another \$19 million obligated for local contractors to
perform work in the next 90 days on these projects," she said. "While
this hold is an inconvenience to us**, [it's a [big]{.mark}ger [burden
for local businesses who want to get paid]{.mark} for work already
completed under these contracts]{.underline}."**

**Geoengineering and other renewable technology failing now --
Indigenous resistance and Trump**

**Corbett '25** \[Jessica; January 30; Senior editor and staff writer;
Common Dreams; "\'Geoengineering Fails Again\': Coalition Cheers End of
Arctic Ice Project";
[[https://www.commondreams.org/news/geoengineering]{.underline}](https://www.commondreams.org/news/geoengineering)\]
jason

Environmental and Indigenous activists declared Thursday that
[\"**[geoengineering fails again]{.mark},\" welcoming the shutdown of a
project that aimed to use \"a reflective material to protect and restore
Arctic sea ice,\" which is rapidly disappearing as humanity\'s reliance
on fossil fuels heats the planet.**]{.underline}

**[Noting that \"we committed to moving forward only if we could
conclusively demonstrate both the safety and effectiveness of our
approach,\" the [A]{.mark}rctic [I]{.mark}ce [P]{.mark}roject team
confirmed in an online statement that it \"will be [concluding its
research]{.mark} and winding down the organization.\"]{.underline}**

**[\"While our climate impact simulations have shown promising results
(with a new scientific paper forthcoming), recent ecotoxicological tests
have revealed potential risks to the Arctic food chain,\" the team said.
\"Our initial approach was to continue research aimed at addressing
these concerns. Upon further reflection, however, the board decided that
the [combination of]{.mark} these new test results with [broad
skepticism]{.mark} toward geoengineering, [resistance to introducing new
materials]{.mark} into the Arctic Ocean, and the [increasingly
challenging funding environment (and paucity of federal research
dollars]{.mark}), the [most realistic path was to conclude]{.mark} our
[research]{.mark}.\"]{.underline}**

\"Nature is not a laboratory; it is a living entity we are in
relationship with.\"

Responding in a Thursday statement, Hands Off Mother Earth Alliance
global coordinator Coraina de la Plaza said that \"the cancellation of
the Arctic Ice Project marks another monumental victory for our planet
and future generations, a victory where Indigenous peoples\' resistance
has been central. This outcome reflects the power of community advocacy,
and while **[the [fight]{.mark} against geoengineering is [far from
over]{.mark}, this is a significant step to continue protecting the
Arctic against industry greed and vested interests.\"]{.underline}**

Panganga Pungowiyi, climate geoengineering organizer at Indigenous
Environmental Network, called the decision \"long overdue.\"

\"We are concerned for the community members in Utqiaġvik who were made
to spread football fields of this material onto their frozen lake. For
years, we stood in defense of Indigenous lands and the sacred ice that
has sustained our communities for generations,\" Pungowiyi explained.
\"Our concerns about the reckless use of harmful materials were
dismissed, yet we knew that the health of our ecosystems and the wisdom
of our people must not be overlooked. We continually showed up in
defense of free prior and informed consent, and made our presence
known.\"

\"We continue to state firmly that nature is not a laboratory; it is a
living entity we are in relationship with,\" the organizer added.
\"While we find relief in this victory, [we remain vigilant against
other forms of geoengineering that threaten our sacred
spaces]{.underline}. Together, we will continue to educate and empower
our communities, standing with our lands, waters, and air for the
generations to come.\"

Silvia Ribeiro, Latin America director at ETC Group, said that \"today
we celebrate the wisdom, experience, and work of Indigenous peoples and
organizations in Alaska that stopped this project and **[[stand in
solidarity]{.mark} with their vigilance [against similar
experiments]{.mark} that are planned in Arctic regions.\"]{.underline}**

Mary Church, geoengineering campaign manager at the Center for
International Environmental Law, also framed the development as \"a huge
victory for the Indigenous communities at the forefront of resistance to
the industries and vested interests that are polluting the planet and
gambling with our collective future.\"

\"Geoengineering approaches do nothing to address the root causes of the
climate crisis and instead delay real solutions, offering a free pass to
polluters,\" she stressed. \"Following the recent reaffirmation of the
global moratorium on geoengineering at the U.N. biodiversity summit in
Colombia, governments need to act to prevent harmful outdoor experiments
and the slippery slope to legitimizing deployment. Instead of betting on
highly speculative techno-fixes, governments must prioritize an urgent
and just transition away from fossil fuels to protect vital Arctic
ecosystems.\"

Benjamin Day, a senior campaigner for climate and energy justice at
Friends of the Earth U.S., also looked to the fight ahead.

\"The [decision to shut down the Arctic Ice Project completes the
Geoengineering Hype Cycle that we now see so often: Entrepreneurs swoop
into local communities claiming they have a solution to global warming,
assuring everyone it\'s completely safe and ignoring the red flags
raised by those with deep knowledge of local ecosystems]{.underline},\"
Day said. \"After countless wasted dollars and press attention, it\'s
revealed the community was right and geoengineering is not a safe or
responsible way to address climate change.\"

\"Collectively,\" he argued, \"we must stop enabling this cycle and work
towards rapidly and equitably transitioning our communities to
sustainable energy and land-use practices.\"

The winding down of the Arctic Ice Project comes amid **[[global fears
about]{.mark} what the recent [return of]{.mark} Republican U.S.
President Donald [Trump]{.mark}---who [cozied up to Big Oil]{.mark}
executives on the campaign trail [and promised to \"drill, baby,
drill,\"]{.mark} despite the devastating impacts of fossil fuels---will
mean for the future of a planet that last year saw record-shattering
temperatures.]{.underline}**

**[Already, [Trump]{.mark} has [ditched]{.mark} the [Paris]{.mark}
climate agreement (again), [lifted]{.mark} a [freeze on]{.mark} new
[l]{.mark}iquefied [n]{.mark}atural [g]{.mark}as exports, [declared a
\"national energy emergency]{.mark},\" [and named]{.mark} various
[fossil fuel allies to key positions.]{.mark}]{.underline}**
Environmental Protection Agency Administrator Lee Zeldin and
Transportation Secretary Sean Duffy took their posts earlier this week,
Interior Secretary Doug Burgum was confirmed by the
Republican-controlled Senate Thursday evening, and Chris Wright,
Trump\'s pick for energy secretary, awaits confirmation.

### AT: Arctic Green Transition Now --- 2NC/1NR

**[Speculative bubbles]{.underline} and lack of [domestic
mining]{.underline} prevent wide scale transition -- Sweden proves**

**Hedlund '25** \[Stefan; April 28; SME, widely published author,
researcher, and lecturer; GIS; "Sweden fumbles its green transition,"
https://www.gisreportsonline.com/r/sweden-fumbles-energy-transition/\]
jason

Inspired by its very own teenage climate change activist Greta Thunberg,
in the past decade **[[Sweden]{.mark} decided to [place]{.mark} a [major
wager on]{.mark} promoting the [green transition]{.mark}. In contrast to
fluffy proclamations about lifestyle changes, Stockholm's strategy was
to be based on the solid application of state-of-the-art technology. The
early stages included the [creation of]{.mark} three new industrial
groupings: [Hybrit]{.mark} (a company built to produce fossil-free
steel), [Northvolt]{.mark} (a company founded to produce batteries for
electric vehicles, or EVs) [and Stegra]{.mark} (a second venture to
produce fossil-free steel). Given the huge amounts of electricity that
would be needed to realize those ventures, the plan for reliable power
supplies went [all-in on]{.mark} a massive expansion of [wind
farms]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

Above all, Sweden's green transition was designed to be a model for the
world to emulate, providing a picture-perfect illustration of what a
well-designed public-private partnership can achieve.

During the early years, the media was captivated by an abundance of
optimistic politicians and entrepreneurs, touting their close
cooperation. In June 2022, then Prime Minister Magdalena Andersson, a
Social Democrat now in the opposition, smilingly informed members of
parliament about "the wave of investment that is currently sweeping
across Sweden, with battery factories in the north and the west, with
fossil-free steel production and with green mining. It is fantastic!"
She concluded by lauding the Green Party for driving the country in the
direction of sustainability. Sweden was now leading the way into a green
future. Or so it seemed.

Entering into green transition dreamland

It kicked off in 2016, with the creation of Hybrit Development AB, a
joint venture formed by state-owned behemoths SSAB (steelmaking), LKAB
(mining) and Vattenfall (power generation). Aiming to fundamentally
transform the iron and steel industry, a unique technology was developed
that would allow the production of fossil-free steel. By using hydrogen
instead of coal in the ore reduction process, emissions would be clean
water instead of dirty carbon. The company envisioned itself being able
to reduce Sweden's total carbon dioxide emissions by at least 10
percent. Realizing that the use of fossil-free steel would greatly
improve their own customer relations, makers of cars, trucks and other
heavy equipment happily jumped on the bandwagon.

A second step was taken in 2020, with the formation of H2 Green Steel,
later to be renamed Stegra. The business proposal was the same as that
of Hybrit, namely, to produce fossil-free steel by using hydrogen
instead of coal. Production was to begin in 2024, with an initial
capacity of 2.5 million tons per year. The plan envisioned that by 2030,
production would rise to double the 2024 volume. Based in the city of
Boden, an old military bastion just 80 kilometers south of the Arctic
Circle, the venture was to create 1,500 jobs.

Another critical step was taken in 2021, when the company Northvolt AB
commissioned its first manufacturing plant in Skelleftea, a bygone
industrial and mining town located on the Swedish coast of the Gulf of
Bothnia. Founded in 2015 by two former Tesla executives, the company
would use lithium-ion technology to produce batteries for electric
vehicles. The vision was that its booming output of batteries would
greatly reduce European dependence on China. In support of this vision,
the company also announced plans for five other plants to be built in
Europe and North America.

As all three ventures were revving up their respective publicity
campaigns, the previously stagnant north of Sweden was transformed into
a veritable Klondike for green investment. Recruitment campaigns were
launched to attract workers, both from Sweden and from abroad. The city
of Skelleftea in particular became a boomtown, where housing could not
be built soon enough to accommodate all the incoming workers.

Calls for prudence drowned out

There had been numerous warning voices in recent years, raising
questions both about the relevance of the proposed technologies (which
had not yet been proven as viable at an industrial scale) and about the
generation sources for the exponential boost in consumption of
electricity that was to come. Those voices were drowned out.

Facts & figures

Sweden's state-of-the-art green transition

The media preferred to go with the hype around the proposed
transformation of the thinly populated north of Sweden into the promised
land for green technologies and sustainability. Those who had raised
concerns were, however, soon to be proven right. **[[In]{.mark} October
[2024, Hybrit was "placed on pause]{.mark}."]{.underline}**

**[Sweden wakes up from its green revolution dream]{.underline}**

**[On [March]{.mark} 12, [2025]{.mark}, it all came to a screeching
halt. Following a long period of attempted rescues that included seeking
Chapter 11 protection in a United States court, [Northvolt]{.mark}
finally [declared bankruptcy]{.mark}. It was the biggest bankruptcy in
Swedish corporate history, involving numerous outside investors and
creditors, most notably major auto manufacturers like Volkswagen that
had banked nearly \$1 billion on Swedish deliveries of batteries for
their own made-in-Europe EVs. Many believe that [Stegra is likely
next]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

The scandal not only provided a sordid illustration of how the
public-private partnership had allowed venture capitalists to get poorly
monitored access to taxpayer money via lavish loan guarantees. Even
worse was that Swedish laws had been changed to enable state pension
funds to invest in new ventures that are not listed on the stock
exchange, a high-risk bet with funds requiring conservative management.

Meanwhile, it has also come to light that the [various windfarm projects
associated with the overall green-transition strategy have been huge
loss-makers.]{.underline}

Implications of the green transition going bust

The losses that have been inflicted on the pension funds alone
constitute a huge scandal. Foreign [auto makers like Volkswagen and BMW
have wound down investment activities, casting doubt on the chosen
technology]{.underline} and commercial strategy. Foreign investors and
sources of green lending have had an equally negative experience. The
city of Skelleftea is in a major crisis, and nobody seems to know what
to do with all the foreign workers who were attracted to settle in the
barren north of Sweden, where their newly bought houses can now only be
sold at huge losses.

Attention is turning to what will be done to clean up the mess. Instead
of showing the world a glorious path into a fossil-free future,
**[Sweden now stands as a [cautioning example]{.mark}, [warning]{.mark}
others of [how]{.mark} unchecked [green activism can produce huge
speculative bubbles that cause widespread damage when they
burst]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

The country now finds itself at a crossroads and will have to decide on
three highly contentious issues, all of which have implications for the
broader European outlook.

Energy generation

The first issue concerns cleaner power generation, which is critical to
all visions of the green transition. When Sweden's nuclear power program
reached its peak in the 1980s and 1990s, with 12 operating reactors, the
country had a completely fossil-free power generation system. Half was
nuclear and half was hydro. A small oil-fired plant was kept in reserve,
as a hedge against sudden spikes in demand. Most importantly, the system
was balanced, with hydro catering to demand in the north and nuclear to
demand in the south.

Driven by a red-green coalition (three center-left to left-wing
political parties), the country proceeded to shut down six of its
nuclear reactors and to **[place a massive wager on expansion of wind
power that has already markedly destabilized the electricity
distribution system. When it is windy, the country exports electricity.
When it is calm, electricity prices go through the roof.]{.underline}**

**[More importantly, when demand in the denuclearized and heavily
populated south is high, transmission capacity from the distant north is
woefully insufficient. This not only leads to huge differences in prices
that are set according to geographic criteria. It also creates technical
problems, as the system is not built to handle large fluctuations
combined with the implied needs for long-distance
transmission.]{.underline}**

The wager on a green transition in the north shone an unforgiving light
on these problems. Given the implied longer-term increase in electricity
demand, and the inability to expand hydropower, the only way out --
short of acknowledging the utility of nuclear power or returning to
fossil fuels − was a formidable wager on wind power, mainly in the form
of huge offshore windfarms that would require additional government
support. The outlook for the latter to materialize is currently shrouded
in great uncertainty.

As the current center-right government has campaigned on a return to
nuclear power, it has taken a decidedly cold attitude to further
taxpayer subsidies for the expansion of wind power. The government has
raised the bar for entry into such ventures by announcing that the state
will not foot the bill for connector cables from offshore wind farms to
the land-based grid. And it has announced a blanket ban of any offshore
windfarms in the central part of the Baltic Sea, citing
military-security concerns that such installations might make it hard to
detect incoming Russian cruise missiles.

[Given that the envisioned return to nuclear power will not materialize
in the short term, Sweden's **[green transition is in
jeopardy]{.mark}**]{.underline}. If it falters for reasons of inadequate
technology, then the problems relating to spikes in demand for
electricity will be alleviated. But if political priorities result in
more public support so the green transition continues, it begs the
question of how to boost power generation effectively and sustainably.

Mining: Not in my backyard!

The **[second strategic issue Stockholm faces concerns mining. Although
Sweden has long been an operator of nuclear power plants and it has
substantial deposits of uranium, the fuel for reactors,
[environmentalists]{.mark} have [refused to allow mining of]{.mark}
those domestic deposits. Instead, they prefer that uranium be imported
from Russia.]{.underline}**

**[The refusal to allow mining concessions becomes even more contentious
with respect to the [r]{.mark}are [e]{.mark}arth [m]{.mark}ineral[s that
are]{.mark} so badly [needed for the green transition]{.mark}. While
Sweden does have substantial deposits of such minerals, green lobbies
have successfully barred their exploration. This [NIMBY]{.mark} ("not in
my backyard") mentality [ensures]{.mark} that [mining]{.mark} for rare
earths, like uranium mining, [takes place far afield]{.mark}, in this
case in China or in poor countries in Africa. The political left's
intransigence prolongs Sweden being beholden to adversarial powers while
stymieing the country's economic development and
security.]{.underline}**

**[Stranded production assets]{.underline}**

**[The third issue concerns what to do with production facilities that
have been built at huge public expense and are currently idle.
Fossil-free steel sounds like a great idea -- but is the technology
sufficiently viable?]{.underline}**

**[The Northvolt factory is still standing and there is hope that
outside investors may still be found. But if [Beijing]{.mark} turns out
to be the [only willing party]{.mark}, then the [hope of producing
sufficient batteries for Europe to reduce its dependence on China will
be defeated]{.mark}.]{.underline}** Stockholm is also likely to cite
security as a rationale to prevent a Chinese takeover of stranded
assets.

**Canada cut NWP renewable efforts, resulting in [entire
programs]{.underline} getting [deleted]{.underline}**

**Williams '25** \[Ollie; March 5; Editor and co-founder; Cabin Radio;
"'No new sources' to replenish Arctic Energy Alliance funds";
[[https://cabinradio.ca/226160/news/politics/no-new-sources-to-replenish-arctic-energy-alliance-funds/]{.underline}](https://cabinradio.ca/226160/news/politics/no-new-sources-to-replenish-arctic-energy-alliance-funds/)\]
jason

The **[[Arctic Energy Alliance]{.mark}, which is funded through the
territorial and federal governments, [offers energy efficiency
programs]{.mark} to northerners and supports small-scale renewable
energy projects.]{.underline}**

**[For example, the group -- also known as the AEA -- has administered
programs helping people to buy wood stoves, evaluate their homes for
energy efficiency improvements like better insulation, install solar, or
acquire rebates on electric vehicles.]{.underline}**

**[But a large chunk of the [AEA's funding recently evaporated]{.mark},
making it [harder to meet demand]{.mark} or keep the same programs in
place.]{.underline}**

In 2019-20, the Arctic Energy Alliance signed on to a federal program
called the Low Carbon Economy Fund that effectively doubled the funding
at the AEA's disposal.

That cash was used to make existing programs better and create some new
ones to fill gaps.

The Low Carbon Economy **[Fund money has now run out and there is [no
indication it will be renewed or replaced]{.mark} by a similar federal
program. That means the AEA is back to "essentially our funding levels
from 2018-19," its boss, Mark Heyck, told MLAs in December last
year.]{.underline}**

**[Heyck said the [absence of]{.mark} that [funding]{.mark} had
[resulted in the closure of]{.mark} popular [programs]{.mark} like those
that helped people in smaller communities to acquire wood
stoves.]{.underline}**

**[Even as the NWT completes its initial corridor of electric charging
stations from Yellowknife to the south, the territory is [not budgeting
any money for e]{.mark}lectric [v]{.mark}ehicle rebates this coming year
-- deleting the \$153,000 it set aside last year.]{.underline}**

In the legislature on Monday, infrastructure minister Caroline Wawzonek
said the GNWT had raised the disappearing federal funding with Ottawa
counterparts.

"I suspect -- given the state of affairs in shift, or likely to be in
shift shortly, with our federal colleagues -- that this is an area we
will have to re-examine once we know what the next government's
iteration of priorities might be in Ottawa," Wawzonek said in response
to a question from Frame Lake MLA Julian Morse, referring to the
likelihood of a forthcoming federal election.

"We put a lot of money into big infrastructure projects in this
department, and I'm not denying that those are important, but a little
bit of money to each individual homeowner goes a long way to helping
them reduce the cost to their pocketbooks, and that's the thing that
people feel when you make changes for them," said Morse.

"People aren't going to notice their costs go down because we put a
bunch of money towards this or that infrastructure project. ... It's
something that I think is an important area. I think it's important to
residents."

Earlier that day, Yellowknife North MLA Shauna Morgan had questioned
Wawzonek on what she said was a view that energy initiatives are "a
luxury we can't afford."

"Wasting energy is a luxury we can't afford," Morgan said.

"In each year's budget we devote more and more millions to energy
subsidies, electricity rate subsidies, seniors' home heating subsidies,
fuel subsidies for communities in crisis such as Norman Wells.

"These offer much-needed relief but they don't address any of the root
causes of high energy costs, leaving the government on the hook for
ever-increasing subsidies. We could reduce the amount of subsidies
needed by ramping up our energy efficiency rebates and incentives."

Morgan gave the examples of a Yellowknife family saving \$6,000 per year
by insulating a 1970s-era trailer, or a family in Fort Liard saving
\$600 annually on water and electricity bills through help to buy more
efficient appliances.

"With sunsets in federal funding we have significantly reduced our
rebate programs, leaving more and more folks choosing inefficient
furnaces, boilers, and appliances because they seem cheaper upfront,"
she said.

"But it's costing them much more in the long run. It's also costing the
climate and costing this government. We are sliding backwards."

Wawzonek did not disagree, saying the loss of federal funding through
the Low Carbon Economy Fund had resulted in a "significant impact."

However, she said the department did not have an obvious means of
replacing that cash and restoring the Arctic Energy Alliance's former
level of service.

**["We [don't have]{.mark} other or additional [independent
funds]{.mark} necessarily to come up with in areas where we could
realign," the minister said.]{.underline}**

**["Without being able to source some additional funding, it's
[difficult to come up]{.mark} at this point in time [with a]{.mark}
simple answer or simple [solution]{.mark}."]{.underline}**

All this occurs in a context where the [**[renewable energy]{.mark},

against fossil fuels]{.mark}. [Countries such as Norway]{.mark}, which
[have traditionally been large oil producers, are]{.mark} [beginning to
diversify]{.mark}**]{.underline} their economies to reduce their
dependence on hydrocarbons.

Norway, for example, has set up a commission to study its energy model,
which concludes that it will not extract all of its hydrocarbon reserves
and that it needs to diversify its Gross Domestic Product (GDP) to
generate other types of wealth, mainly from renewable sources. This
phenomenon is being replicated in other oil-producing countries, as
increasing efficiency and competition in the renewable energy sector is
making crude oil extraction in complex areas such as the Arctic
increasingly less profitable.

La International Energy Agency (IEA) has warned that investment in
renewable energy must be tripled if carbon reduction targets are to be
met and global temperature rise kept below 1,5°C. This paradigm shift is
directly affecting the fossil fuel industry in the Arctic.

**[[Could the Arctic be the turning point]{.underline}]{.mark}** in
energy geopolitics?

**[[With]{.mark} increasing pressure to reduce carbon emissions and
[the]{.mark} unstoppable [rise of renewable energy]{.mark}, [the Arctic
has]{.mark} [gone from being]{.mark} a [hydrocarbon-rich]{.mark} region
[to a battlefield]{.mark} in the fight against climate change. [The
transition to a]{.mark} [green]{.mark}er [economy]{.mark} [is]{.mark}
not only [affecting]{.mark} large [fossil fuel
corporations]{.mark}]{.underline}**, but also the geopolitical powers
that depend on these resources.

Russia, for example, with its vast Arctic territory, has increased its
investment in infrastructure in the region, building gas pipelines and
offshore platforms in an attempt to capitalise on its natural resources.
However, international sanctions, as a result of the conflict in
Ukraine, are affecting its ability to attract foreign investment that is
key to the development of these projects.

On international agreements such as the High Seas Treaty and the
geographical restrictions imposed by some banks such as the Santander,
also play an important role in the governance of the region. These
treaties seek to limit the exploitation of Arctic resources in
international zones, while bank policies restrict the financing of new
fossil fuel projects in the region.

On the other hand, **[[companies and gov]{.mark}ernment[s]{.mark} [that

will]{.mark} likely [decline]{.mark}]{.underline}** as countries move

https://magazine.scienceforthepeople.org/geoengineering/best-case-scenario-carbon-underground/,

The downside is that it's currently very expensive, with estimates
ranging between \$100 and \$600 per ton of carbon removed depending on
the energy source used. So while direct [[air
capture]{.underline}]{.mark} seems to have more potential to remove
carbon at climate-significant scales than BECCS, far more work is needed
to refine the technology.

All of these **[[tech]{.mark}nologies [require]{.mark} both [more
funding for r]{.mark}esearch [and d]{.mark}evelopment [as well
as]{.mark} strong policy incentives to mature, whether that means a
[robust price on carbon or gov]{.mark}ernment [pollution
controls]{.mark}. On the bright side, perhaps [this means they
are]{.mark} more likely to be an extension or complement to [reducing
carbon output]{.mark}]{.underline}** rather than a substitute for it.
Without heavy state intervention, it is impossible to imagine CCS

capacity of geological reservoirs is sufficient that there aren't
geological limits to carbon sequestration. **[Eventually, there would be
systemic limits for inputs to CCS---land in the case of bioenergy;
renewable energy supply in the case of direct air capture---and so the
yearly rate of carbon sequestration would be constrained.]{.underline}**

This means that a full carbon-clean up---that is, if the world wanted to
bring greenhouse gas concentrations back to pre-industrial levels, as a
restoration or recompensation project---could stretch over centuries.
But the near-term practical constraints on CCS are grounded only by
political will.

In a best-case scenario, carbon capture and sequestration would be the
world's most massive pollution clean-up operation, conducted as a public
service. But if the public is paying for it, there's an obvious problem:
**[[most of the tech]{.mark}nology [for removing carbon]{.mark}
currently [lies in the hands of fossil fuel polluters]{.mark} who are
already experienced in it via enhanced oil recovery
(EOR).]{.underline}**

With EOR, a well operator injects something---heat, chemicals, water, or
gas like carbon dioxide---into an oil well that's been depleted, which
basically flushes the residual oil out, extending the life of the well.
Perversely from a climate change standpoint, carbon dioxide is currently
being mined for use in enhanced oil recovery from natural sources in
Colorado and elsewhere, and piped to areas like West Texas for oil
recovery. The US already has a 4,500-mile network of CO2 pipelines
primarily for this purpose,13 and the carbon dioxide supply still can't
meet the demand of the oil producers.

**[Links---Geoengineering---2NC/1NR]{.underline}**

**Geoengineering will [dramatically reduce the demand]{.underline} for
fossil fuels, causes decreased investment in the fossil fuel industry**

**Sustainability Directory 25** --- founded by Lorne Michael Cousins,
who holds Practitioner status at the Institute of Environmental
Management and Assessment (IEMA) in the UK and the program director for
The Conference Board\'s China Sustainability Leaders Council, to offer a
knowledge hub on ESG topics, 4/26/25, The Sustainability Directory, "Can
Geoengineering Reduce Reliance on Fossil Fuels?"
https://energy.sustainability-directory.com/question/can-geoengineering-reduce-reliance-on-fossil-fuels/,
igo

**[[Geoengineering]{.underline}]{.mark}**, sometimes termed climate
intervention, encompasses a range of proposed large-scale techniques
aimed at deliberately altering the Earth's climate system to counteract
the impacts of climate change. The fundamental premise often involves
manipulating radiative forcing or removing greenhouse gases from the
atmosphere. Within the context of energy in sustainability,
understanding these methods requires recognizing their distinct aims.
Geoengineering is primarily concerned with managing climate symptoms or
greenhouse gas levels, rather than directly transforming the source of
energy production or consumption patterns.

There are two principal categories into which geoengineering proposals
broadly fall. The first involves Solar Radiation Management (SRM),
methods intended to reflect sunlight back into space, thereby cooling
the planet. Stratospheric aerosol injection, cloud brightening, or
space-based reflectors exemplify SRM approaches.

The second category focuses on Carbon Dioxide Removal (CDR), which seeks
to extract carbon dioxide already present in the atmosphere. Examples
include afforestation and reforestation, bioenergy with carbon capture
and storage (BECCS), direct air capture (DAC), enhanced weathering, and
biochar.

Considering if geoengineering [can [**reduce reliance** **on fossil
fuels**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Meaning → Fossil fuels denote combustible
materials originating from geological strata, formed over eons from the
remains of prehistoric organisms. necessitates examining its indirect
effects on energy systems. Climate change itself intensifies energy
reliance in certain ways. Rising global temperatures increase demand for
cooling, particularly in already warm climates, putting additional
strain on electricity grids that often still rely on fossil fuel
sources. Extreme weather events, made more frequent and intense by
climate change, disrupt energy infrastructure, requiring resilient,
often more distributed, energy systems.

**[[Geoengineering]{.mark} techniques aim to alter the climate system,
not replace energy sources.]{.underline}**

**[SRM methods, by potentially moderating global temperatures,
[might]{.mark} hypothetically [temper the increase in]{.mark} [cooling
demand]{.mark}. [A slower rate]{.mark} of warming [could translate
to]{.mark} a [less]{.mark}急 [need for air conditioning]{.mark} capacity
growth [powered by fossil fuels]{.mark}]{.underline}** in some regions.
This is an indirect link; the method itself does not generate energy or
change how energy is produced. It merely attempts to reduce one driver
of future energy demand increase linked to climate change.

**Arctic FNPPs substantially decrease fossil fuel industry investment**

Vladimir **Aptekarev** 20**24** (Deputy CEO for Shipbuilding, Floating
Power Units and Marine Engineering at Rosatom's Machine Engineering
Division) May 24, 2024. "Q&A: The prospects for floating nuclear power
plants,"
https://www.world-nuclear-news.org/Articles/Q-A-The-prospects-for-floating-nuclear-power-plant#:\~:text=At%20the%20present%20moment%2C%20land,supply%20is%20not%20interrupted%20for-
CG

At the present moment, land-based nuclear power plants and **[[floating
power units]{.mark} represent two separate businesses. We do not sell
floating power units; instead, we sell electricity generated by them. As
a result of technical and economic feasibility studies, scientific
research, and extensive market research in foreign countries, we have
[manage]{.mark}d to adopt an extremely economically efficient and, at
the same time, highly convenient principle of operation for our clients.
This involves supplying electricity based on Power Purchase Agreements
(PPAs) with highly predictable costs, extending up to 60 years,
depending on the preferences and capabilities of the customer, [without
being tied to the cost or dependency on hydrocarbons]{.mark}. However,
electricity supply is not interrupted for the entire duration of the
contract, up to 60 years, which is the maximum projected operational
lifespan of the floating power unit. This is achieved through the use of
the RITM-200M, optimisation of the fuel cycle, with a refueling interval
of 7-10 years, and the presence of a spare floating power unit in the
operational \"energy fleet\" during the refueling and planned
maintenance period when the installed power unit is sent to a
specialised base in the Russian Federation. [Seven power units operating
worldwide]{.mark}, regardless of the country, [can be]{.mark}
[substituted by just one floating power unit.]{.mark} [Such a]{.mark}
[model]{.mark} is not only economically efficient but also [eliminates
the need for handling nuclear fuel]{.mark} in the host country of the
floating power unit, [following the \"green field\" principle]{.mark}.
Additionally, [it]{.mark} [obviates the need]{.mark} for the foreign
partner to create expensive infrastructure.]{.underline}**

### Links --- FNPPs --- 2NC/1NR

**The plan displaces fossil fuels**

**1AC WNA 24**. World Nuclear Association, "Nuclear Power and Energy
Security," 2024,
https://world-nuclear.org/information-library/economic-aspects/nuclear-power-and-energy-security

**[Reliable, long-term electricity supply -- [nuclear]{.mark} power
plants [provide]{.mark} [large amounts of electricity]{.mark}
predictably, with reactors achieving high average capacity factors.
Plants built today will operate for 60 or more years. As the
[electricity]{.mark} from nuclear plants is reliable, nuclear can
[directly displace fossil fuels]{.mark}]{.underline}** from a country's
electricity mix, reducing a country's import dependency and diversifying
its energy mix.

Energy density -- Uranium is a uniquely concentrated source of energy
which is easily and cheaply transportable. The quantities needed are
very much less than for coal or oil; 30 tonnes of fabricated nuclear
fuel are required per year for a 1GW power plant compared with over 3
million tonnes of coal. It is therefore intrinsically a very portable
and tradeable commodity. Critically, this means that it is easy to
establish strategic inventories; fuel supply for up to two years is
typically stored at power plants.

Geographical and political availability -- Uranium is relatively
abundant and fuel supplies are spread among politically diverse
countries (see below), reducing the risk of supply disruptions.

**[Predictable costs -- fuel costs for nuclear plants are a minor
proportion of total generating costs. The cost [of electricity]{.mark}
supplied from nuclear plants [is]{.mark} therefore
[predictable]{.mark}]{.underline}** over the course of a plant's
operational lifetime.

Resilient infrastructure -- nuclear plants are built to withstand and
continue operating in extreme weather, as proven during hurricanes and
freezing temperatures in the United States and other places.

Ancillary grid services -- nuclear plants can contribute substantially
to the provision of ancillary services such as flexible operation to aid
frequency control and provision of grid inertia. Nuclear can also help
assimilate variable renewable energy without increasing dependency on
imported natural gas.

**[Heat and power -- for most major industrial heat applications,
nuclear energy is the only credible non-carbon option. Using
[nuclear]{.mark} for process heat can [reduce demand]{.mark} for [oil
and]{.mark} natural [gas]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

**Plan replaces fossil fuel power plants in the Russian Arctic.**

**[In general, [SMRs]{.mark} are [designed]{.mark} [for]{.mark}

**[FNPP's]{.underline} destroy the fossil fuel industry --- eliminates
reliance on fossil fuels**

**NEI et al. '23**, Nuclear Energy Institute, "It's a Bird. It's a
Plane. It's a Floating Nuclear Power Plant," 8-9-2023,
https://www.nei.org/news/2023/its-a-bird-its-a-plane-its-floating-nuclear,
\-\--nws

Spurring Versatile Power

**[[FNPPs]{.underline}]{.mark}** allow for a wide range of versatile
power applications. Developers construct them on ships or barges as
self-contained, mobile power stations, ensuring they can deploy them to
virtually any coastal region or waterway. This flexibility opens new
possibilities **[for generating clean energy]{.underline}** in areas
that were previously inaccessible for traditional nuclear facilities.
FNPPs can be an ideal solution to meet the specific energy demands of
these regions, such as island nations that need energy for a wide
variety of applications including water desalination, district
heating/cooling, and electricity. Additionally, developers can build
FNPPs with ready-made infrastructure like shipyards.

By utilizing modular construction techniques to construct smaller,
simpler reactors, developers reduce costs while paving the way for the
quick deployment of nuclear power at sea.

**[Decarbonizing Heavy Industries]{.underline}**

Addressing climate change **[[will require]{.underline}]{.mark}**
**[[significantly reducing]{.underline}]{.mark}** our greenhouse gas
emissions, especially from **[[heavy industries
that]{.underline}]{.mark}** have historically **[[relied on fossil
fuels.]{.underline}]{.mark}** FNPPs can be deployed near coastal
industrial complexes, minimizing transmission losses and providing a
stable power supply to energy-intensive operations. This localized power
generation helps reduce the strain on existing power grids and assures a
consistent energy supply to critical industries. Companies are also
planning on deploying nuclear reactors on civilian maritime vessels for
power and propulsion. Ocean transportation carries more than 80 percent
of the world's exported goods via large diesel engines.

If these vessels were a country, they would be sixth globally on
greenhouse gas emissions---just above Germany. Next-generation nuclear
is the only zero-emission energy source available to power these vessels
that make up the backbone of global shipping,

Fostering Innovation.

This isn't nuclear's first time at sea---in fact, our nation has a rich
history of using nuclear energy to support our naval operations. Though
the technologies used by the Navy and proposed floating nuclear plants
differ, they both showcase the innovation that the nuclear industry is
known for. The United States developed the world's first nuclear-powered
submarine, the USS Nautilus, which first went to sea in 1955. In 1958,
the Nautilus set another record when it became the first submarine to
traverse under the North Pole. Six decades later, we now have the
world's largest nuclear-powered navy, which has traveled over 130
million miles using nuclear power (enough to circle the Earth 3,500
times!). From shaping the flexible energy systems of tomorrow, to
advancing decarbonization across the economy,
**[[FNPPs]{.underline}]{.mark}** are an innovative solution that can
accelerate progress toward our climate goals. Learn more about how
nuclear energy **[[creates opportunities for industries to reduce or
eliminate their carbon footprint]{.mark}]{.underline}** ---shaping a
brighter future on land and at sea.

**Countries model the plan's FNPPs once they see it works, replacing
fossil fuels beyond the Arctic.**

**1AC** Jonathan **Szeto** 20**20** (Air Quality researcher at the
University of Pennsylvania) May 21, 2020. "The Potential and Challenges
of Off-Shore Nuclear"
https://kleinmanenergy.upenn.edu/commentary/blog/the-potential-and-challenges-of-off-shore-nuclear/

was complete by October of 2018 costing Russia about 7.631 billion

electricity and 300 MW of heat; [enough to supply 200,000 people with
energy]{.mark}. Other [countries]{.mark} such as Indonesia, Malaysia,
Cape Verde, and Argentina [have shown interest]{.mark} in purchasing
similar plants from Rosatom.]{.underline}**

**[[China has]{.mark} also shown great [interest in this
tech]{.mark}nology and in May 2014, the China Atomic Energy Authority
(CAEA) signed an agreement with Rosatom to construct FNPPs for China's
offshore islands. These FNPP are being built in China but are [based on
