https://academic.oup.com/oocc/article/5/1/kgaf011/8099165?login=false#511047316

**[[Biodiversity is the fabric of life]{.mark}]{.underline}** on Earth
**[[and the life support system that underpins planetary health and
human well-being]{.underline}]{.mark}** \[289\]. **[[Fossil fuels pose
an existential threat to]{.underline}]{.mark}** the planet's
**[[biodiversity]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Fossil fuels drive the [climate
crisis]{.underline} which is **[[accelerating species extinction risk
and ecosystem degradation, and cause biodiversity harms through fossil
fuel development and petrochemical pollution]{.underline}]{.mark}**. In
[a destructive cycle, the **[fossil-fuel-driven degradation of wild
places and loss of species worsens the climate
crisis]{.mark}**]{.underline} by disrupting ecosystem-based carbon
dioxide removal from the atmosphere, which is needed to curb global
heating \[290\]. Overall, [fossil-fuel-driven biodiversity loss has
direct and dire impacts on food security and nutrition, water quality
and availability, health and well-being, carbon storage and
sequestration, resilience to climate change,]{.underline} and almost all
of nature's vital contributions \[291\], on top of the incalculable,
[non-monetizable costs of species extinctions]{.underline}.

[Worldwide an estimated one million animal and plant species are
threatened with extinction]{.underline}, with [climate
change]{.underline} as a primary [driver, alongside habitat destruction
and exploitation]{.underline} \[292\]. Scientists have long documented
the widespread and growing stresses of anthropogenic climate change on
species and ecosystems, as rising temperatures, more extreme weather
events, coastal flooding, sea ice and glacier melt, and other climate
hazards make conditions more inhospitable. [Climate change is disrupting
species' distributions, timing of breeding and migration, physiology,
vital rates, and genetics]{.underline} \[293--296\]. It is spreading
disease and invasive species; [increasing mortality and localized
extinctions; and deteriorating ecosystem processes]{.underline}
\[297--299\]. Climate change-related local extinctions are already
widespread \[300\].

[**[Every increase in fossil fuel pollution]{.mark}** and resulting
climate stress pushes ecosystems **[closer to tipping points and
amplifies extinction risk]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [With ∼1.5°C
temperature rise as of 2024, the world's coral reef
ecosystems]{.underline}---[which support]{.underline} a quarter of ocean
life and the [livelihoods of a half billion people---are in
crisis]{.underline} due to increasingly frequent and severe bleaching
events from ocean heating paired with ocean acidification \[301\]. [Half
of the world's reefs have already been lost \[302\] and near total
global reef collapse is projected at 2°C temperature rise]{.underline}
\[66\]. An estimated [1.6% of species are projected to become extinct at
the current level of temperature rise]{.underline} \[303\]. Numerous
[studies]{.underline} have [project]{.underline}ed [catastrophic species
losses with continued fossil fuel pollution]{.underline} with, for
example, the [climate-related extinction of 14%--32% of animal and plant
species]{.underline}---representing the [potential loss of 3 million to
6 million species]{.underline}---[in]{.underline} the [next 50 years,
even under intermediate fossil fuel emissions scenarios]{.underline}
\[304\]. Scientists have called for the urgent transformation of our
energy system away from fossil fuels to prevent a mass extinction event
\[305\].

[**[Fossil fuel development]{.mark}** itself]{.underline} also [causes a
wide array of harms]{.underline} to species and ecosystems:
**[[destroying and fragmenting wildlife habitat, causing air, noise, and
light pollution, contaminating surface and ground water and reducing
water supplies]{.underline}]{.mark}**, and **[[facilitating the spread
of ecologically disruptive invasive species]{.underline}]{.mark}**
\[306--308\], with similar harms in the marine environment \[309\].
[Fossil fuel infrastructure creates]{.underline} the [significant
**[risk of oil spills]{.mark}** and brine spills,]{.underline} which can
[kill wildlife and cause long-term impacts over large areas]{.underline}
\[310\]. The fossil fuel-based energy system has led to mortality,
changes in behavior, and population declines in many species,
disruptions to community composition, and loss of ecosystem function
\[311, 312\].

### Impact Turn --- Warming --- 2AC

#### Strong fossil fuel industry bad --- warming

Manon **Laudy 25,** Communications lead at Greenpeace, Media
Correspondent with the Fossil Free Revolution, writer for Greenpeace
International, 4/25/25, "Fossil fuels are a threat to our security
here's 6 reasons why,"
https://www.greenpeace.org/international/story/74236/fossil-fuels-are-a-threat-to-our-security-heres-6-reasons-why/
//maeve

**[5[. Fossil fuels worsen climate chaos and exacerbate extreme
weather]{.mark}]{.underline}**

**[[Fossil fuels are the number one driver of climate change]{.mark},
responsible for around 75% of global greenhouse gas emissions. And
just 36 fossil fuel companies are responsible for half of global
CO2 emissions. [Their polluting]{.mark} business has [contributed to at
least 12,000 extreme weather events]{.mark} in the past 50 years,
killing two million people, with lowest income countries bearing 90% of
this unimaginable loss.]{.underline}**

**[[To avoid the worst]{.mark} climate [chaos,]{.mark} [we must switch
off fossil fuels and their]{.mark} climate wrecking
[pollutants.]{.mark}]{.underline}**

**Warming = extinction**

**Kemp et al 22** --- Lead researcher at The Centre for the Study of
Existential Risk, and professor at the University of Cambridge,
Cambridge. (Luke, Chi Xu, Joanna Depledge, Kristie
L. Ebi, Goodwin Gibbins, Timothy A. Kohler,
Johan Rockström, Marten Scheffer, Hans
Joachim Schellnhuber, Will Steffen, and Timothy M. Lenton; "Climate
Endgame: Exploring catastrophic climate change scenarios," Proceedings
of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America,
8/1/22, https://www.pnas.org/doi/full/10.1073/pnas.2108146119) sh

[Prudent risk management requires consideration of bad-to-worst-case
scenarios.]{.underline} Yet, for climate change, such potential futures
are poorly understood. Could anthropogenic [**[climate change result in
worldwide societal collapse or even eventual human extinction]{.mark}**?
At present, this is a dangerously underexplored topic. Yet **[there are
ample reasons]{.mark} to suspect that climate change could result in a
global catastrophe**]{.underline}. Analyzing the mechanisms for these
extreme consequences could help galvanize action, improve resilience,
and inform policy, including emergency responses. We outline current
knowledge about the likelihood of extreme climate change, discuss why
understanding bad-to-worst cases is vital, articulate reasons for
concern about catastrophic outcomes, define key terms, and put forward a
research agenda. The proposed agenda covers four main questions: 1) What
is the potential for climate change to drive mass extinction events? 2)
What are the mechanisms that could result in human mass mortality and
morbidity? 3) What are human societies\' vulnerabilities to
climate-triggered risk cascades, such as from conflict, political
instability, and systemic financial risk? 4) How can these multiple
strands of evidence---together with other global dangers---be usefully
synthesized into an "integrated catastrophe assessment["? It is time for
the scientific community to grapple with the challenge of better
understanding catastrophic climate change.]{.underline} How bad could
climate change get? As early as 1988, the landmark Toronto Conference
declaration described the ultimate consequences of climate change as
potentially "second only to a global nuclear war." Despite such
proclamations decades ago, climate catastrophe is relatively
under-studied and poorly understood.

The potential for catastrophic impacts depends on the magnitude and rate
of climate change, the damage inflicted on Earth and human systems, and
the vulnerability and response of those affected systems. [**[The
extremes]{.mark}** of these areas, such as **[high temperature rise and
cascading impacts]{.mark}**, are underexamined]{.underline}. As noted by
the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), there have been
few quantitative estimates of global aggregate impacts from warming of
3 °C or above (1). Text mining of IPCC reports similarly found that
coverage of temperature rises of 3 °C or higher is underrepresented
relative to their likelihood (2). Text-mining analysis also suggests
that over time the coverage of IPCC reports has shifted towards
temperature rise of 2 °C and below. Research has focused on the impacts
of 1.5 °C and 2 °C, and studies of how climate impacts could cascade or
trigger larger crises are sparse.

A thorough risk assessment would need to consider how risks spread,
interact, amplify, and are aggravated by human responses (3), but even
[simpler "**[compound hazard]{.mark}**" analyses of interacting climate
hazards and drivers are underused. Yet this is how risk unfolds in the
real world.]{.underline} For example, a cyclone destroys electrical
infrastructure, leaving a population vulnerable to an ensuing deadly
heat wave (4). Recently, [we have seen compound hazards emerge between
climate change and the COVID-19 pandemic]{.underline} (5). As the IPCC
notes, [**[climate risks are becoming more complex and difficult to
manage, and are cascading across regions and sectors]{.mark}**
(6).]{.underline}

Why the focus on lower-end warming and simple risk analyses? One reason
is the benchmark of the international targets: the Paris Agreement goal
of limiting warming to well below 2 °C, with an aspiration of 1.5 °C.
Another reason is the culture of climate science to "err on the side of
least drama" (7), to not to be alarmists, which can be compounded by the
consensus processes of the IPCC (8). Complex risk assessments, while
more realistic, are also more difficult to do.

This caution is understandable, yet it is mismatched to the risks and
potential damages posed by climate change. We know that temperature rise
has "fat tails": low-probability, high-impact extreme outcomes (9).
C[limate damages are likely to be nonlinear and result in an even larger
tail (10). Too much is at stake to refrain from examining high-impact
low-likelihood scenarios.]{.underline} The COVID-19 pandemic has
underlined the need to consider and prepare for infrequent, high-impact
global risks, and the systemic dangers they can spark. Prudent risk
management demands that we thoroughly assess worst-case scenarios. Our
proposed "Climate Endgame" research agenda aims to direct exploration of
the worst risks associated with anthropogenic climate change. To
introduce it, we summarize existing evidence on the likelihood of
extreme climate change, outline why exploring bad-to-worst cases is
vital, suggest reasons for catastrophic concern, define key terms, and
then explain the four key aspects of the research agenda. Worst-Case
Climate Change

Despite 30 y of efforts and some progress under the United Nations
Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC) anthropogenic greenhouse
gas (GHG) emissions continue to increase. [Even without considering
worst-case climate responses, the current trajectory puts the world on
track for a temperature rise between 2.1 °C and 3.9 °C by
2100]{.underline} (11). If all 2030 nationally determined contributions
are fully implemented, warming of 2.4 °C (1.9 °C to 3.0 °C) is expected
by 2100. Meeting all long-term pledges and targets could reduce this to
2.1 °C (1.7 °C to 2.6 °C) (12). [Even **[these optimistic assumptions
lead to dangerous Earth system trajectories]{.mark}**. Temperatures of
more than 2 °C above preindustrial values have not been sustained on
Earth's surface since before the Pleistocene Epoch]{.underline} (or more
than 2.6 million years ago) (13).

Even if anthropogenic GHG emissions start to decline soon, this does not
rule out high future GHG concentrations or extreme climate change,
particularly beyond 2100. [There are **[feedbacks]{.mark}** in the
carbon cycle **[and]{.mark}** potential **[tipping points]{.mark}** that
could generate high GHG concentrations]{.underline} (14) that are often
missing from models. [Examples include Arctic permafrost thawing that
releases **[methane]{.mark}** and CO2 (15), carbon loss due to intense
droughts and fires in the Amazon (]{.underline}16), and the apparent
slowing of dampening feedbacks such as natural carbon sink capacity (17,
18). These are likely to not be proportional to warming, as is sometimes
assumed. [Instead, abrupt and/or **[irreversible changes]{.mark}** may
be triggered at a temperature threshold. Such changes are evident in
Earth's geological record, and their impacts cascaded across the coupled
climate--ecological--social system (19). Particularly worrying is **[a
"tipping cascade]{.mark}**" in which multiple tipping elements interact
in such a way that tipping one threshold increases the likelihood of
tipping another]{.underline} (20). Temperature rise is crucially
dependent on the overall dynamics of the Earth system, not just the
anthropogenic emissions trajectory.

### Impact Turn --- Democracy --- 2AC

#### Strong Fossil Fuel Industry erodes democracy---fosters authoritarian rise. 

Allison **McManus et al.** 20**23** (Managing Director, National
Security and International Policy of the Center of American progress,
Chris Martinez, Laura Kilbury, Joel Martinez, Calee White, Mariel Lutz,
Kat So, Kate Petosa, Anne Christianson) Dec. 5, 2023. "These Fossil Fuel
Industry Tactics Are Fueling Democratic Backsliding"
https://www.americanprogress.org/article/these-fossil-fuel-industry-tactics-are-fueling-democratic-backsliding/#:\~:text=Fossil%20fuel%20companies%20and%20trade,the%20United%20States%20and%20abroad.-
CG

[Through a wide array of tactics, **the [multinational \$4 trillion
fossil fuel industry has]{.mark} not only corrupted citizens'
understanding of the climate crisis but also [contributed to the erosion
of democracy around the world.]{.mark} [First, by]{.mark} propagating
deception and [misinfo]{.mark}rmation, the [industry has polluted the
info]{.mark}rmation ecosystems [essential for democracies to
function]{.mark}, pushing open societies closer to the "post-truth"
politics that [allow authoritarianism to thrive]{.mark}.
[Second,]{.mark} enabled in part by its misinformation campaigns, the
[industry]{.mark} has [used its considerable financial and lobbying
influence to bend policy]{.mark} to serve its own narrow interests
instead of the public good. [Finally]{.mark}, the fossil fuel industry
[has]{.mark} directly [undermined core democratic rights]{.mark} by
filing unfounded lawsuits to silence critics, promoting draconian
anti-protest laws, and supporting voter suppression**]{.underline}
efforts.

**Democratic Backsliding causes global war**

Benjamin **Appel &** Sarah **Croco 24**, Appel is a researcher whose
work focuses on the effectiveness of intergovernmental organizations and
international law in world politics. He is particularly interested in
how the United Nations Security Council affects the peaceful resolution
of interstate crises. He also has projects on the International Criminal
Court and its impact on human rights and domestic conflict, as well as
research on state repression. He previously worked as an associate
professor of political science at Michigan State University, Croco is an
Assistant Professor in the Department of Government and Politics at the
University of Maryland, 5/30/24, "Democratic Backsliding and Foreign
Policy",
https://ucigcc.org/wp-content/uploads/2024/05/Appel_working-paper_final.pdf
//maeve

**[What [we have seen with]{.mark} many [backsliding states, leaders are
able to consolidate power through]{.mark} completely [legal
means]{.mark}]{.underline}** (e.g., appointing new judges, changing
electoral laws, etc.). They can also work to manipulate elements outside
of political institutions---for example, owning or otherwise controlling
the national media. 2 For example, Mansfield and Snyder (1995);
Mansfield and Snyder (2009); Cederman et al. (2010); though see Narang
and Nelson (2009). 3 For example, Weeks (2008) and Weeks (2012). 4 For
example, Colgan and Weeks (2015); Croco and Weeks (2016); Croco (2011).
IGCC Working Paper No. 2 \| May 2024 4 This means the institutions
themselves remain intact---multiple branches of government still exist
and the rule of law still holds in theory---but the leader faces far
less accountability than a prototypical democratic leader. While this
has myriad domestic downsides, it also, as we argue here, has
international implications[. **[If leaders have a free hand]{.mark}
domestically, [this allows them to engage in risky foreign]{.mark}
policy [interactions]{.mark} or other aggressive pursuits. It also means
the likelihood of leaders being punished for bad foreign policy outcomes
is greatly reduced. [This further emboldens the leader]{.mark} to
challenge the international status quo.**]{.underline} To test our
argument, we empirically assess whether backsliding states engage in
more hostile interactions including both verbal (i.e., diplomatic
condemnation) and military (i.e., military show of force) with other
states compared to robust democracies.5 **[We also recognize the
[potential for endogeneity]{.mark} given the nonrandom nature [of
backsliding]{.mark}. To alleviate these concerns, we utilize a new fixed
effects counterfactual estimator6 that accounts for this issue. Our
results support our theory and remain consistent even when subjected to
several robustness checks. Our findings constitute an important step
forward in understanding the complex relationship between a state's
domestic regime attributes and its international behavior. [The
consequences of backsliding are not limited to the domestic
sphere]{.mark}. Indeed, [the effects often spill over]{.mark} into the
international realm [in the form of threats, aggression, or even
physical violence]{.mark}. The rest of this paper proceeds as follows.
Section 2 presents our theory as to why democratic [backsliding should
lead to aggressive international behavior]{.mark}. First, we briefly
discuss how backsliding leaders work to centralize power and reduce
domestic accountability. [We then explore how these changes]{.mark} in
the domestic sphere [encourage leaders to stir up discord with other
states]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Section 3 outlines our research design,
while Section 4 presents our empirical findings. Section 5 concludes
with a discussion of the implications of our findings and potential
avenues for future research.

### XT --- Democracy I/L --- 1AR

**Strong Fossil Fuel industry [destroys]{.underline} public trust and
democratic consensus\-\--multiple tactics.**

Allison **McManus et al**. 20**23** (Managing Director, National
Security and International Policy of the Center of American progress,
Chris Martinez, Laura Kilbury, Joel Martinez, Calee White, Mariel Lutz,
Kat So, Kate Petosa, Anne Christianson) Dec. 5, 2023. "These Fossil Fuel
Industry Tactics Are Fueling Democratic Backsliding"
https://www.americanprogress.org/article/these-fossil-fuel-industry-tactics-are-fueling-democratic-backsliding/#:\~:text=Fossil%20fuel%20companies%20and%20trade,the%20United%20States%20and%20abroad.-
CG

The **[[fossil fuel industry's spread of deceptive]{.mark} or misleading
[info]{.mark}rmation [about climate change]{.mark}---often referred to
as disinformation and misinformation---[hurts democracies by undermining
public trust in gov]{.mark}ernment[s]{.mark}, [democratic
processes]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} even the notion of [objective
truth]{.mark} itself. Within the climate change discussion, the fossil
fuel [industry]{.mark}---whose emissions are the dominant cause of
global warming---has [made]{.mark} a [coordinated]{.mark}
[effort]{.mark} [to]{.mark} [spread]{.mark} climate
[disinfor]{.mark}mation and misinformation that prevents essential
action to address climate change. Through a sophisticated lobbying and
public influence machine targeting lawmakers and citizens, the fossil
fuel [industry]{.mark} has [undemocratically]{.mark} [shape]{.mark}d
[how the world thinks]{.mark} and talks about the climate
crisis.]{.underline}**

**[In particular, ["greenwashing"---]{.mark}when an entity depicts
itself as doing more on climate than it actually is---has become a
frequent industry [tactic]{.mark} to [distract from]{.mark} and delay
[meaningful proposals to reduce emissions]{.mark}. Greenwashing not only
[harms the public's ability to be well-informed]{.mark}; it also [erodes
public trust in scientific]{.mark} [consensus and genuine
solutions]{.mark} to the climate crisis. For instance, oil and gas
[companies]{.mark} often [tout]{.mark} their [investments]{.mark} [in
biofuels]{.mark} and hydrogen as evidence that they are taking action on
climate, [but]{.mark} these investments [are]{.mark} largely
[superficial]{.mark}]{.underline}** and vastly outweighed by the
continued reliance on fossil fuels that the industry perpetuates.

Industry's declared efforts to fight climate change fall woefully short,
with **[oil and gas companies often [devoting more attention to]{.mark}
creating the [appearance]{.mark} of working on climate solutions than
actually developing them.]{.underline}**

Fossil fuel companies greenwash their harmful practices not only with
direct advertising but also through their trade associations. Trade
associations effectively serve as public relations vehicles, touting the
so-called environmental commitments of the fossil fuel industry while
downplaying the environmental harms their core products cause. For
instance, the American Petroleum Institute (API) regularly publicizes
its member companies' investments in renewable energy and carbon
reduction technologies. On closer inspection, however, industry's
declared efforts to fight climate change fall woefully short, with oil
and gas companies often devoting more attention to creating the
appearance of working on climate solutions than actually developing
them.

**[Similar to greenwashing, the fossil fuel [industry]{.mark} also
[avoids]{.mark} [accountability]{.mark} [through "fossil fuel
solutionism"]{.mark} and "technological optimism," [creating]{.mark} the
[appearance of investing in low-carbon solutions]{.mark} while, in
reality, the industry expands fossil fuel production and carries on with
obstruction of meaningful climate policy. Individuals within the oil and
gas industry have bluntly admitted that [proposals]{.mark} such as these
[serve]{.mark} more [as ways to "shut up whoever we need to shut]{.mark}
[up"]{.mark} than to actually solve the climate crisis. Internal
communications from major fossil fuel companies show similar
contradictions]{.underline}**---such as Shell's guidance to employees
urging them "not give the impression that Shell is willing to reduce
carbon dioxide emissions to levels that do not make business sense."

[**Another influence [tactic]{.mark} used by the fossil fuel industry
and its allies is ["astroturfing,"]{.mark} by which they [create]{.mark}
an [artificial]{.mark} [appearance of grassroots support for]{.mark} an
[industry-friendly policy while concealing]{.mark} the support's
[true]{.mark} [origins]{.mark}. For example**,]{.underline} the
anti-offshore wind organization American Coalition for Ocean Protection
[(ACOP)]{.underline} [is a project of the Caesar Rodney Institute (CRI),
a think tank based in Delaware that has received funding from both
fossil fuel interests and the apparent astroturf group Protect Our Coast
NJ]{.underline}. Two CRI executives manage ACOP's Ocean Environment
Legal Defense Fund, the goal of which is to create a permanent offshore
wind exclusion zone. However, with [many of these groups also supporting
offshore oil drilling]{.underline}, their [opposition to wind farms
seems intended simply to slow down progress on this renewable competitor
to fossil fuels]{.underline}.

**[The oil and gas [industry]{.mark}'s public influence methods are
becoming increasingly sophisticated as they [hire major p]{.mark}ublic
[r]{.mark}elations [firms to promote]{.mark} their [false
narrative]{.mark}s and generate astroturf groups.]{.underline}**

**[Fossil fuel [companies]{.mark} and trade groups promote climate
obstruction through additional indirect methods, including by
[fund]{.mark}ing [think tanks and political candidates who deny the
reality of climate change]{.mark}. By casting doubt on climate science
and [portraying a false picture of scientific
debate]{.mark}]{.underline}**---even though oil and gas companies have
internally known about the climate threat for decades---[these companies
delay meaningful climate action]{.underline}.

The **[fossil fuel industry's influence campaigns do more than simply
mislead the public about climate change and oil and gas companies'
ostensible efforts to fight it; they also foment extreme false beliefs
on the issue. For example, the industry promotes fabricated narratives
such as warnings that proponents of climate action are supposedly
threatening to destroy individual liberty with a "climate lockdown."
Such [rhetoric serves to hyperpolarize]{.mark} the issue of [climate
change]{.mark} [into]{.mark} another front in the [culture war]{.mark}s,
[undermining societies' ability to reach]{.mark} productive, [democratic
consensus both in the]{.mark} [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [and
abroad]{.mark}. Around the world, far-right parties and candidates have
made opposition to climate action a major component of their populist
agenda.]{.underline}**

**[[With]{.mark} its [disproportionate financial and lobbying
influence]{.mark}, the fossil fuel [industry manipulates democratic
functions to undermine popular views]{.mark} and hamstring climate
action proposals that would serve the public interest. In the United
States alone, the fossil fuel industry spends tens of millions of
dollars to influence policymakers to keep the system rigged for itself.
So far in 2023, oil and gas companies and associations have spent almost
\$93 million on lobbying; and in 2022, it was more than \$125
million.]{.underline}** Between lobbying, political contributions, and
advertising efforts, these interests spent 27 times as much as climate
advocacy groups from 2008 to 2018 to obstruct climate action.

**Abusive fossil fuel industry undermines democratic
freedoms\-\--efforts include substantial lawsuits, anti protest
legislation, and voter suppression efforts.**

Allison **McManus et al**. 20**23** (Managing Director, National
Security and International Policy of the Center of American progress,
Chris Martinez, Laura Kilbury, Joel Martinez, Calee White, Mariel Lutz,
Kat So, Kate Petosa, Anne Christianson) Dec. 5, 2023. "These Fossil Fuel
Industry Tactics Are Fueling Democratic Backsliding"
https://www.americanprogress.org/article/these-fossil-fuel-industry-tactics-are-fueling-democratic-backsliding/#:\~:text=Fossil%20fuel%20companies%20and%20trade,the%20United%20States%20and%20abroad.-
CG

**[While polluting public discourse and manipulating governments
represent indirect attacks on democratic systems, the [fossil fuel
industry]{.mark} and its allies further [strengthen]{.mark} their
[influence by directly undermining the fundamental rights]{.mark} at the
heart [of free societies]{.mark}. They do this in [three main ways:
1)]{.mark} by [filing]{.mark} expensive, protracted strategic lawsuits
against public participation [(SLAPPs); 2)]{.mark} by [pushing
anti-protest legislation]{.mark}; and [3)]{.mark} by [promoting voter
suppression]{.mark} policies.]{.underline}**

[**In response to the 2016 Standing Rock protests in the United States,
Dakota Access Pipeline operator Energy Transfer Partners filed a
[SLAPP]{.mark} against protester[s]{.mark} to [prevent similar
demonstrations from spreading]{.mark}. Other instances abound, with
fossil fuel companies often misusing anti-racketeering Racketeer
Influenced and Corrupt Organizations (RICO) laws against protesters
simply to "send a message**,]{.underline}" even when they know their
chances of winning these lawsuits are slim.

[Similar examples of such lawsuits exist outside the United
States]{.underline}. In [Italy, for example, oil company Eni has
similarly deployed lawsuits against protesters]{.underline} and fossil
fuel dissenters [to delay climate action]{.underline}: In [July 2023,
Eni filed a SLAPP against Greenpeace Italy and ReCommon, and in 2021,
the company filed a claim against Italian newspaper]{.underline} Il
Fatto Quotidiano [for defamation.]{.underline}

The **[fossil fuel [industry's attempts]{.mark} to undermine democratic
freedoms also [include support for anti-protest laws that seek to
silence public criticism by criminalizing protest activity.]{.mark} As a
result of these efforts, some U.S. states have enacted anti-protest laws
that carry severe sentences for vague, speech-chilling
charges]{.underline}** such as attempting to "obstruct, impede, or
impair the services or transmissions of an energy facility."

Meanwhile, [in the United Kingdom, Prime Minister Rishi Sunak has
implemented both the Public Order Act of 2023 and the Policing, Crime,
Sentencing and Courts Act (PCSA), which undermine fundamental freedoms
to assemble and demonstrate]{.underline}. [Right-wing think tank Policy
Exchange, **which is funded by [major fossil fuel companies]{.mark} and
their allies, [help]{.mark}ed [draft]{.mark} the [legislation]{.mark}.
Since the passage of the PSCA, numerous U.K. climate activists,
particularly those operating under the Just Stop Oil umbrella, have been
arrested for their protest activities.**]{.underline}

**[The oil and gas industry's voter suppression efforts are directly
connected to its goal of thwarting the fight against climate
change.]{.underline}**

**[The fossil fuel [industry]{.mark} has [also played]{.mark} a [major
role in promoting voter suppression]{.mark}, which disproportionately
affects voters who most strongly favor climate and environmental justice
policies. [Between 2015 and 2020,]{.mark} fossil fuel interests [donated
millions to state lawmakers who backed voter suppression
bills]{.mark}.]{.underline}** This [support includes funding the
organizations leading the voter suppression charge. For example, fossil
fuel giant Koch Industries is a key funder of organizations such as the
Honest Elections Project, a spinoff of the right-wing American
Legislative Exchange Council]{.underline} (ALEC), [which]{.underline}
[has been]{.underline} instrumental in [pushing voter suppression and
other anti-democratic proposals]{.underline}.

These voter suppression efforts are directly connected to the fossil
fuel industry's goal of thwarting the fight against climate change.
Notably[, **voter suppression disproportionately disenfranchises
communities of color, low-income groups, and Indigenous
peoples---communities that favor environmental regulation and climate
change legislation significantly more than other demographics. Because
such groups are also more likely to vote for candidates who support
climate justice, [suppressing]{.mark} their [votes]{.mark} can help
[prevent]{.mark} the [election of lawmakers that favor real policy
proposals on climate change]{.mark}.**]{.underline}

### XT --- Democracy Impact --- 1AR

**Democracy substantially reduces the risk of global war, while
authoritarianism increases it**

Markus **Jaeger 25**, Senior Adviser at the Atlantic Council, adjunct
professor at Columbia University, Fellow of the German Council on
Foreign Relations, and a part of the Freedom and Prosperity Center,
3/24/25, "Why democracies stick together: The theory and the empirics
behind alliance formation",
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/in-depth-research-reports/report/why-democracies-stick-together-the-theory-and-empirics-behind-alliance-formation/
//maeve

**[[Democratic peace theory posits that democracies do not go to
war]{.mark} with one another, while [democratic alliance theory
suggests]{.mark} that [they rarely]{.mark}, if ever, [join nondemocratic
alliances]{.mark} (or alliances led by nondemocratic powers). [Empirical
evidence strongly supports both theories. The Atlantic Council's Freedom
and Prosperity Indexes further reinforces]{.mark} the hypothesis
[that]{.mark} economically prosperous democracies tend to align with one
another, whereas [authoritarian states gravitate toward similarly
nondemocratic]{.mark} and less prosperous
[partners.]{.mark} ]{.underline}**

If these theories hold, they carry significant implications for Western
and especially US foreign policy. **[First, [global democratization
would reduce the number of potential conflicts]{.mark}, at least among
an increasing number of democracies. Second, [it would expand the pool
of democratic alliance partners]{.mark}, while at the same limiting the
alliance options available to nondemocratic powers. [This strategic
logic underscores the importance of upholding democratic norms abroad
and promoting democratization in nondemocratic
states.]{.mark} ]{.underline}**
